B000009RC2|5.0|I first heard of the Mahavishnu Orchestra through reading about leader John McLaughlin's role in Miles Davis' masterpieces [[ASIN:B000002696 Bitches Brew]] and [[ASIN:B00074CBFG A Tribute to Jack Johnson]]. Whilst Miles Davis' three fusion masterpieces becomes successively harder, heavier and more intense, they do not prepare a listener for what McLaughlin was to achieve upon leaving Miles to form his own band, named the Mahavishnu Orchestra after his association with a Hindu guru.<br /><br />Despite its title, \\"The Inner Mounting Flame\\" is anything but devotional music. This is, in its essence, jazz made completely electric, with Jan Hammer's keyboards, Rick Laird's bass and the violin of Jerry Goodman providing the \\"waves of melody\\" that give hints to where such bands as [[ASIN:B0000019LR the Dirty Three]] would travel in the 1990s. This is apparent from the intense, yet tuneful opening track \\"Meeting of the Spirits\\" that established the classic Mahavishnu sound of distorted guitar and bass over melodic violin and keyboards and Bill Cobham's remarkably precise drumming. Even though electric, the Mahavishnu Orchestra's music is so free-flowing that any alert listener will recognise it as jazz rather than as rock.<br /><br />The following two tracks, though much faster and less emotional, have similar sorts of quiet-to-loud dynamics that would be perfected by Brian McMahan and Dave Pajo on [[ASIN:B0000019HU Spiderland]] two decades later. This is most apparent on \\"Noonward Race\\", where McLaughlin and Laird play their instruments in squalls that are too melodic to be noise, but too intense to be a melody even in the sense of an album like [[ASIN:B000002LRJ Loveless]]. The fourth track, \\"A Lotus on Irish Streams\\" is a true anomaly here, sounding more like [[ASIN:B00042YJJ2 Popol Vuh]] than most of the other songs here. Hammer's piano, though, makes the piece far from ordinary.<br /><br />The classic Miles Davis groove of \\"Vital Transformation\\" puts things back in their familiar place after the detour of the fourth track, and with Goodman's violin taking the place of Davis' trumpet, \\"Vital Transformation\\" sounds more like Miles than anything else on \\"The Inner Mounting Flame\\" - and has every quality that made Davis' first electric works so unique. \\"The Dance of Maya\\" is creepy and slower than anything else here, but it really will haunt the aware listener with the amazing accuracy of every player over the first two minutes and forty seconds. The rest of \\"The Dance of Maya\\" is lighter and faster - true jazz that moves so freely the players sound as if they are playing just because they enjoy it.<br /><br />The first half of \\"You Know, You Know\\" features McLaughlin on acoustic guitar, which he plays in a tense, brooding manner that leaves one imagining the music will become louder long before the fierce squalls of Goodman's violin disturb the calm (if only momentarily) in a way never duplicated by anyone. The closer, \\"Awakening\\", in contrast, is ferocious right from the very first notes and never lets up through its relatively short three minute and thirty-two second running time. The fierce passion of this piece summarises a record in which McLaughlin manages to perfect completely free-form electric music.<br /><br />It is because all five members play with such freedom that they are able to achieve an emotional level that never lets up even on the quietest moments in a manner impossible in structured songs where players have to restrain themselves to play the structures correctly. \\"The Inner Mounting Flame\\" is thus a landmark: one that would in the 1990s be copied without acknowledgement by post-rock groups but whose tremendous power needs to be heard firsthand.he detour of the fourth track, and with Goodman's violin taking the place of Davis' trumpet, \\"Vital Transformation\\" sounds more like Miles than anything else on \\"The Inner Mounting Flame\\" - and has every quality that made Davis' first electric works so unique. \\"The Dance of Maya\\" is creepy and slower than anything else here, but it really will haunt the aware listener with the amazing accuracy of every player over the first two minutes and forty seconds. The rest of \\"The Dance of Maya\\" is lighter and faster - true jazz that moves so freely the players sound as if they are playing just because they enjoy it.    The first half of \\"You Know, You Know\\" features McLaughlin on acoustic guitar, which he plays in a tense, brooding manner that leaves one imagining the music will become louder long before the fierce squalls of Goodman's violin disturb the calm (if only momentarily) in a way never duplicated by anyone. The closer, \\"Awakening\\", in contrast, is ferocious right from the very first notes and never lets up through its relatively short three minute and thirty-two second running time. The fierce passion of this piece summarises a record in which McLaughlin manages to perfect completely free-form electric music.    It is because all five members play with such freedom that they are able to achieve an emotional level that never lets up even on the quietest moments in a manner impossible in structured songs where players have to restrain themselves to play the structures correctly. \\"The Inner Mounting Flame\\" is thus a landmark: one that would in the 1990s be copied without acknowledgement by post-rock groups but whose tremendous power needs to be heard firsthand.
B0018LMZOO|4.0|This is my final review of a 2008 remastered \\"core 7\\" Moody Blues cd.  Seventh Sojourn follows in the tradition and downcast mood of the previous album Every Good Boy Deserves Favour from 1971.  However, on Seventh Sojourn, there was a light at the end of the tunnel.  The much needed break for the band was in sight.  I don't think any of them knew how long a rest they would need.  But, the melancholy of EGBDF was followed by a spiritual awakening of sorts for the band on SS.  It would become clear on the individual songs of Seventh Sojourn.  There is an optimism present, even though the sadness over the shape of the world ran deep.  On EGBDF, they were just so fatigued and had no time to be pensive.  They had no opportunity to think clearly.  On Seventh Sojourn, there is a lot of introspection and soul-searching in the songs.  I love this album, even though I will oftentimes favor listening to the more peppy AQOB or the last cd of studio wizardry in TOCCC.  I will end my last review of a \\"core 7\\" 2008 remaster with a list of my favorites.  They are the following:<br /><br />1. A Question of Balance<br />2. To Our Children's Children's Children<br />3. Days of Future Passed<br />4. In Search of the Lost Chord<br />5. Seventh Sojourn<br />6. Every Good Boy Deserves Favour<br />7. On The Threshold of a Dream<br /><br />Don't get me wrong!  I love all seven of the core albums in each of their own unique ways!  I think the list is determined more by how often I will listen to a cd, more than the degree to which I like it.  They all seven deserve their own merits and are all worthy.  I can't wait until the next round of remasters from Octave to Keys of the Kingdom are released.  They all six need to be remastered, each to a varying degree, but I look forward to reviewing them when the time comes!<br /><br />Sincerely, Rick Whiteley
